The alternative to nomadic Turkmen troops was [mamluks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mamluks "Mamluks"). While also of Turkic and often nomadic origin, dependence on pasturelands was non-existent for mamluks as they did not live a nomadic life. Previously, mamluks had constituted the later ['Abbasid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/%27Abbasids "'Abbasids"), the [Samanid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samanids "Samanids") and the [Ghaznavid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghaznavids "Ghaznavids") armies. In fact, the Ghaznavid dynasty was itself of mamluk origin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeacock2015225_124-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeacock2015225-124">[122]</a></sup>

The process of mamluk recruitment are well known from other periods in [Islamic history](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_history "Islamic history"), but there is almost no information directly relating to the Seljuks. The chief source of mamluks was most probably forays to the steppe. The alternative to raids was buying them from slave traders and various dealers as evidenced from a slave dispute between a merchant and [Muhammad I Tapar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad_I_Tapar "Muhammad I Tapar").<sup id="cite_ref-125"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup>

### Military of successor states [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Military of successor states")

Many depictions of military figures are known from the period immediately following the Seljuk Empire (which ended in 1194), as illustrated manuscripts started to enjoy a major boom from circa 1200.<sup id="cite_ref-AG_126-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-AG-126">[124]</a></sup> Seljuk styles of military equipment continued during the 13th century in the Turkic post-Seljuk successor states (generally included under the term "Seljuk period"), such as the [Seljuk Rums](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Rums "Seljuk Rums"), the [Zengids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zengids "Zengids"), the [Artuqids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artuqids "Artuqids") or the [Khwarizmians](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khwarazmian_Empire "Khwarazmian Empire"), and are documented in their manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-EI_Clothing_127-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-EI_Clothing-127">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup>

-   Battle scene, in _[Varka and Golshah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Varka_and_Golshah "Varka and Golshah")_, mid-13th century [Seljuk Anatolia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum "Sultanate of Rum")
    
-   Turkic _amir_ with guards in _[Maqamat al-Hariri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maqamat_of_Al-Hariri_(manuscript) "Maqamat of Al-Hariri (manuscript)")_, wearing the _[sharbush](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sharbush "Sharbush")_ headgear, the three-quarters length robe, and boots, at [Rayy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray,_Iran "Ray, Iran"), Iran, 1227.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHillenbrand2010118,_note_10_129-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHillenbrand2010118,_note_10-129">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AC_130-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-AC-130">[128]</a></sup>
    
-   Horsemen duel in _Varka and Golshah_, mid-13th century Seljuk Anatolia
    

## Architecture [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Architecture")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sultan_Sanjar_mausoleum_cropped.jpg)

[Mausoleum of Sultan Sanjar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tomb_of_Ahmad_Sanjar "Tomb of Ahmad Sanjar") (1152) in [Merv](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Merv "Merv"), Turkmenistan<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001146_131-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001146-131">[129]</a></sup>

[Mosques](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mosque "Mosque") and [madrasas](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madrasa "Madrasa") were created and embellished during the period of Seljuk control. Congregational mosques were either repaired, re-built, or constructed in their entirety. The Seljuk sultan also commissioned numerous madrasas to promote the teaching of orthodox Islamic sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_132-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:6-132">[130]</a></sup> These developments in architectural practice are coherent with the Seljuk dynasty's focus on Islam and the promotion of Muslim orthodoxy, the combining of [Sufism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sufism "Sufism") and [Sunnism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunni_Islam "Sunni Islam").<sup id="cite_ref-:6_132-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:6-132">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:9_20-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:9-20">[19]</a></sup> Overall, the architecture attributed to the Seljuk period is characterized by elaborate decoration, much like the other arts produced under Seljuk rule.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_133-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:10-133">[131]</a></sup> Decoration was primarily executed in elaborate brickwork and in the use of colorful glazed tiles.<sup id="cite_ref-:249_134-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:249-134">[132]</a></sup> The most important innovations of this period occurred in the form of mosques, as first seen in the renovations of the [Friday mosque of Isfahan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friday_mosque_of_Isfahan "Friday mosque of Isfahan").<sup id="cite_ref-:0522_135-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:0522-135">[133]</a></sup> One was the introduction of the [four-iwan plan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Four-iwan_plan "Four-iwan plan"). This was attested in some earlier buildings, but under the Seljuks it turned into a common characteristic of mosques, madrasas, and caravanserais in Iran and Central Asia, eventually influencing architecture in Syria, Mesopotamia, and Anatolia as well.<sup id="cite_ref-:0522_135-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:0522-135">[133]</a></sup> Another major innovation was the creation of monumental domes over the space in front of the _[mihrab](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mihrab "Mihrab")_ (or the _[maqsura](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maqsurah "Maqsurah")_), which also became characteristic of later mosques in this region and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-:0522_135-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:0522-135">[133]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Muqarnas_of_Imam_Ali_Mosque_16.jpg)

[Muqarnas](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muqarnas "Muqarnas") in an [iwan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iwan "Iwan") of the Friday mosque of Isfahan (early 12th century)<sup id="cite_ref-:172_93-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:172-93">[91]</a></sup>

Another architectural form that flourished during the Seljuk period was the [muqarnas](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muqarnas "Muqarnas"), a form of three-dimensional geometric decoration.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_136-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:7-136">[134]</a></sup> Some interpretations maintain that the earliest known examples of muqarnas were constructed during the period of Seljuk hegemony, though it also remains possible that they were being developed at the same time in [North Africa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Africa "North Africa").<sup id="cite_ref-:7_136-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:7-136">[134]</a></sup> The layering of multiple embellished cells with divergent profiles in muqarnas creates a dome that has a seemingly-insubstantial interior.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_136-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:7-136">[134]</a></sup> The play of light on the surface enhances this visual effect.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_136-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:7-136">[134]</a></sup> Art historian [Oleg Grabar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oleg_Grabar "Oleg Grabar") argues that the effect of muqarnas domes embodies Qur'anic water symbolism.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_136-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:7-136">[134]</a></sup> Examples of muqarnas also appear in the niches of [mosques](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mosque "Mosque") built during the Seljuk empire.<sup id="cite_ref-137"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup>

## Arts [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Arts")

Various art forms were popularized during the Seljuk period, as evidenced by the vast amount of surviving artifacts.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_20-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:9-20">[19]</a></sup> Most Seljuk arts are known to have been produced in what is modern-day Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_132-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:6-132">[130]</a></sup> However, the Seljuk sultans also encouraged artists to settle in Anatolia as part of a recolonization and reconstruction process of several cities.<sup id="cite_ref-138"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> Many works of Seljuk art continued to be produced following the decline of the empire in the late 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_132-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:6-132">[130]</a></sup> In this regard, the timeline associated with the production of Seljuk art does not entirely match the political events pertaining to the empire and its eventual fall.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_20-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:9-20">[19]</a></sup> Nonetheless, relatively little art can be correctly dated and ascribed to a Great Seljuk context. Much of the material deemed to be Seljuk in world museums in fact belongs to the period A.D. 1150–1250, after the fall of the Great Seljuk Empire, when there seems to have been a sudden burst in artistic production, apparently to a great extent unrelated to court patronage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeacock2015187_139-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeacock2015187-139">[137]</a></sup>

### Ceramics [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Ceramics")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mina%27i_bowl,_dated_1187_CE_-_Muharram,_583_A.H._Iran,_Kashan,_LACMA.jpg)

A _[Mina'i](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mina%27i "Mina'i")_ bowl, dated 1187 CE ([Muharram](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muharram "Muharram") 583 [A.H.](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anno_Hegirae "Anno Hegirae")), a few years before the end of the Seljuk Empire in 1194. Scene of poetic recitation, with poetic verses inscribed on the rim: "If the beloved leaves me, what am I to do? If s/he does not see the wisdom of our union, what am I to do?". [Kashan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashan "Kashan"), Iran. ([Los Angeles County Museum of Art](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Los_Angeles_County_Museum_of_Art "Los Angeles County Museum of Art").)<sup id="cite_ref-140"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup>

Among other ceramics, the manufacture of polychrome ceramic tiles, often used as decor in architecture, were popularized during the Seljuk dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_141-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:8-141">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:11_142-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:11-142">[140]</a></sup> The Seljuks pioneered the use of the _[Mina'i](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mina%27i_ware "Mina'i ware")_ technique, a painted and enameled polychrome overglaze for ceramics.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_142-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:11-142">[140]</a></sup> The glazes on the Seljuk ceramics produced often ranged from a brilliant turquoise to a very dark blue.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_141-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:8-141">[139]</a></sup> The art of Seljuk mosaic tile decorating would continue to dominate the interior of many Anatolian mosques following the period of Seljuk rule.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_141-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:8-141">[139]</a></sup> The Seljuks also created ceramic house models, while other ceramic forms in the Seljuk period included pottery figurines, some of them children's toys.<sup id="cite_ref-143"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup>

-   Bowl with _Majlis_ scene by a pond, by [Abu Zayd](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Zayd_ibn_Mu%E1%B8%A5ammad_ibn_Ab%C4%AB_Zayd "Abū Zayd ibn Muḥammad ibn Abī Zayd"), Iran, dated 1186, MMA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016112,_object_40_144-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016112,_object_40-144">[142]</a></sup>
    
-   [Lustreware](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lustreware "Lustreware") great basin signed by Abu Zayd al-Kashani in 1191, Kashan, Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-145"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup>
    
-   Bowl with enthroned figure and horsemen (Late 12th, early 13th centuries, Iran).<sup id="cite_ref-metmuseum.org_146-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-metmuseum.org-146">[144]</a></sup>
    

### Book arts [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Book arts")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pages_from_a_Seven-part_Quran,_Iran,_late_11th_cen._Khalili_Collection.jpg)

Pages from a Seven-part Quran, Iran, late 11th cen. Khalili Collection

Both secular and non-secular [manuscripts](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manuscript "Manuscript") were produced during the Seljuk period.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_148-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:5-148">[146]</a></sup> These pieces are now limited in availability, considering their ultimate susceptibility to damage overtime.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_149-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:4-149">[147]</a></sup> But those manuscripts that have survived over the centuries provide insight into the Seljuk's involvement in the arts of the book.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_149-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:4-149">[147]</a></sup> [Calligraphers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calligraphy "Calligraphy") and illuminators were responsible for the creation of these manuscripts, though sometimes calligraphers mastered the art of both writing and illustration.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> By the end of the 10th century, both illuminators and calligraphers were beginning to employ various colors, styles, and writing techniques in the realm of the book arts.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup>

The [Qur'an](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quran "Quran")'s produced during the period of Seljuk rule evidence developments in calligraphy and other changes in how the holy text was divided.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> Uniquely, calligraphers during this period frequently combined several scripts on one page of the Qur'an, such as [Kufic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kufic "Kufic") and New Style.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> In addition to these changes in the text, the dawn of the Seljuk empire coincided with a newfound increase in the popularity of paper as a replacement for [parchment](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parchment "Parchment") in the Islamic world.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup> The use of durable paper increased the production of compact, single-volume Qur'an's, whereas parchment codexes often contained multiple volumes of Qur'anic text.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_152-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:0-152">[150]</a></sup> Despite this development, parchment would remain popular for the production of some Qur'an's, and multi-volume pieces continued to be produced.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> Illuminated borders continued to distinguish the Qur'ans produced during the Seljuk period and relative consistency was maintained with regard to their structure.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_152-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:0-152">[150]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:D%C4%ABw%C4%81n_Lugh%C4%81t_al-Turk_(original).jpg)

[Early world map](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_world_map "Early world map") from _[Dīwān Lughāt al-Turk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/D%C4%ABw%C4%81n_Lugh%C4%81t_al-Turk "Dīwān Lughāt al-Turk")_ ("Compendium of the languages of the Turks"), a Turkish-Arab dictionary by the [Kara-Khanid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate "Kara-Khanid Khanate") author [Mahmud al-Kashgari](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahmud_al-Kashgari "Mahmud al-Kashgari"), written in Seljuk Baghdad in 1072-74 CE (1266 copy).<sup id="cite_ref-153"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup>

One example of a manuscript created during Seljuk rule is a thirty-volume ([juz](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juz%27 "Juz'")) Qur'an created c.1050, produced by only one calligrapher and illuminator (Freer Gallery of Art, District of Columbia, F2001.16a-b).<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> As paper had just been introduced to the Islamic world, this piece is an early Islamic paper manuscript.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup> This Qur'an is bound in brown leather, dyed in pink, decorated with gold, and offers an intricate [frontispiece](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Book_frontispiece "Book frontispiece").<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> These elements imply the care that went into the production of this text and indications of frequent usage confirm that it was appreciated.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> It is primarily written in the vertical "New-Style" [Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic "Arabic") script, a sharp, vertical script.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup> The dominant use of New Style in this [folio](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folio "Folio"), also referred to as "new [Abbasid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate "Abbasid Caliphate") Script", attests to the shift from the geometric Kufic script to a more legible calligraphic style, which occurred in the 10th century.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup> Scattered remnants of Kufic, used primarily to indicate volume and page number, also appear in the text.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup> The verticality of the paper in this manuscript speaks to the historic shift away from the horizontal use of paper in many Qur'ans, also a 10th-century development.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup>

Another example of a religious manuscript produced closer to the end of the period of Seljuk Rule is the _Qarmathian Qur'an_ (dispersed folio, Arthur M. Stackler Gallery of Art, District of Columbia, S1986.65a-b).<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> This manuscript's folios are illuminated with a gold border and thin, spiraled illustration, featuring vegetal motifs.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> Despite the generous illumination, the four lines of Qur'anic text on the folio are exceptionally legible.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> Created between the years 1170–1200, this particular folio demonstrates the evolution of New Style, as both vocalized cursive and diacritical dots appear in this later version of the script.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> Only during the 13th century would New Style be replaced by the curvier proportional scripts for regular use.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Book_of_Fixed_Stars_(Kit%C4%81b_suwar_al-kaw%C4%81kib_al-%E1%B9%AF%C4%81bita)_by_%E2%80%9BAbd_al-Rahman_ibn_%E2%80%9BUmar_al-%E1%B9%A2%C5%ABf%C4%AB,_dated_1125_AD_-_manuscript_Doha_Museum_of_Islamic_Art_MS.2.1998_Orion_1.jpg)

A Seljuk manuscript on astrological figures: _[Book of Fixed Stars](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Book_of_Fixed_Stars "Book of Fixed Stars")_ (Kitāb suwar al-kawākib al-ṯābita), by ‛Abd al-Rahman ibn ‛Umar al-Ṣūfī, dated 1125 CE, Baghdad (controlled by the Seljuks from 1055 to 1135).<sup id="cite_ref-154"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup> Doha Museum of Islamic Art [MS 2.1998](https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A2uwar_al-kaw%C4%81kib_(al-th%C4%81bitah)_(DMIA,_MS_2.1198) "commons:Category:Kitāb Ṣuwar al-kawākib (al-thābitah) (DMIA, MS 2.1198)").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016196,_Fig.78_155-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016196,_Fig.78-155">[153]</a></sup>

A final example of a Seljuk Qur'an that has entered into scholarship is a manuscript studied in-depth by the late art historian [Richard Ettinghausen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Ettinghausen "Richard Ettinghausen").<sup id="cite_ref-:5_148-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:5-148">[146]</a></sup> This piece was written in 1164 by Mahmud Ibn Al-Husayn and contains the entirety of the Qur'an (University of Pennsylvania Museum of Archaeology and Anthropology, Philadelphia, NEP27).<sup id="cite_ref-:5_148-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:5-148">[146]</a></sup> Unlike the two Seljuk Qur'ans discussed prior, this manuscript primarily contains [Naskh](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naskh_(script) "Naskh (script)") script, another early Arabic script that replaced Kufic.<sup id="cite_ref-156"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup> However, some Kufic calligraphy is embedded in the chapter headings.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_148-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:5-148">[146]</a></sup> This aspect speaks to how the inclusion of Kufic in Qur'ans became more of a decorative element overtime, often included in headings as opposed to the main body of text.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_151-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:2-151">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> The manuscript is large, with seventeen lines of text per two-hundred and fifteen sheets of paper.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_148-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:5-148">[146]</a></sup> Though not all of the Qur'an is illuminated, both the beginning and the end boast elaborate illustration, with blue, gold, and white hues.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_148-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:5-148">[146]</a></sup> Ettinghausen describes the subsequent visual effect as "brilliant".<sup id="cite_ref-:5_148-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:5-148">[146]</a></sup> The inscriptions feature detailed rosettes, vines, medallions, and [arabesques](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabesque "Arabesque"), some exclusively as decoration and others to indicate the end of particular lines of Qur'anic text.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_148-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:5-148">[146]</a></sup>

Manuscript production during the Seljuk period was not limited to religious texts. Beyond these religious manuscripts, scientific, literary, and historical pieces were created.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_133-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:10-133">[131]</a></sup> One example of a secular manuscript is the _Nusrat al-fatrah_, a historiographical and literary account of the Seljuk period written in 1200 by Imād al-Dīn (Al-Furqan Islamic Heritage Foundation, London).<sup id="cite_ref-157"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the scientific manuscripts produced during the Seljuk period oftentimes pertained to geography, physics, mechanics, mathematics, and astronomy.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_133-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:10-133">[131]</a></sup> The former Seljuk city of [Isfahan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isfahan "Isfahan") not only boasted twelve libraries that contained a total of twelve thousand volumes, but also had an observatory where scholars could record their astrological findings.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_133-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:10-133">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup> Secular manuscripts from the Seljuk empire bear illuminations that often relate to the alignment of planets and the [zodiac](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zodiac "Zodiac"), a couple examples of common themes.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_152-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:0-152">[150]</a></sup>

Whether secular or non-secular, Seljuk illuminated manuscripts had enough influence as to inspire other relevant art forms, such as brass or bronze metal objects.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> For example, the large _Qarmathian Qur'an_ influenced some of the inscriptions on Seljuk ceramic wares.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_150-14"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:3-150">[148]</a></sup> Even mirrors, candlesticks, coins, and jugs manufactured in [Anatolia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatolia "Anatolia") during the Seljuk period would often bear [occult](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occult "Occult") astrological images inspired by manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup> Occult knowledge persisted in manuscripts produced after the decline in the Seljuk's political power in the late 12th century, as the Seljuk sultanate's influence on the book arts continued in Anatolia.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kitab_al-Dariyaq,_folio_24_(royal_court_detail).jpg)

The first known illustrated manuscript of _[Kitâb al-Diryâq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kit%C3%A2b_al-Diry%C3%A2q "Kitâb al-Diryâq")_, is dated 1198, around the end of the Seljuk dynasty, and is generally attributed to the [Jazira](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia "Upper Mesopotamia") (northern [Syria](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria "Syria") or Northern [Iraq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq "Iraq")).<sup id="cite_ref-OP_158-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-OP-158">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CLOTHING_159-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-CLOTHING-159">[157]</a></sup>

Historian Andrew Peacock demonstrates an interest in the Seljuks of Anatolia's focus on occult themes and its manifestation in the book arts.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup> Peacock describes this finding as something that challenges the reigning view that the Seljuks were exclusively the "pious defenders of Islam" when it came to larger systems of belief.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup> Some of the occult sciences that the Seljuks took special interest in included [geomancy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geomancy "Geomancy"), [astrology](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrology "Astrology"), [alchemy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alchemy "Alchemy"). A relevant occult manuscript from a later period of Seljuk influence in the 13th century is the _Dustur al-Munajjimin_, otherwise known as the "Rules of Astrologers", while another is the _[Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq](https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Daq%C4%81%CA%BEiq_al-%E1%B8%A4aq%C4%81%CA%BEiq;_Kit%C4%81b-i_M%C5%AB%CA%BEnis_al-%CA%BFAv%C4%81rif_(BNF_Persan_174) "commons:Category:Daqāʾiq al-Ḥaqāʾiq; Kitāb-i Mūʾnis al-ʿAvārif (BNF Persan 174)")_, or the "Fine Points of Eternal Truths", dating to the [Sultanate of Rum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum "Sultanate of Rum") in 1272.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup> The latter text captures an interest in magic and spells, with a particular focus on calling upon spiritual beings, such as angels, through ritualistic acts (Bibliothèque nationale de France, Paris, Persan 174).<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup> The text was written by a man who wrote under a pen name, "Nasiri".<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup> Interestingly, Nasiri's _Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq_ challenges prevailing Islamic understandings of God while encouraging piety and invoking both [Sufi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sufism "Sufism") terms and themes.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup> For example, while incorporating a Sufi poem, the occult text speaks of supernatural bodies and disputes what Islam considers to be the accepted number of names for God.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_147-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:1-147">[145]</a></sup>

#### Illustrated manuscripts [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Illustrated manuscripts")

The western area of the Seljuk realm including Syria, [Jazira](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jazira_region "Jazira region") and Iraq saw an "explosion of figural art" from the 12th to 13th centuries, particularly in the areas of decorative art and illustrated manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-AG_126-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-AG-126">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnelders20103_160-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnelders20103-160">[158]</a></sup> This occurred despite religious condemnations against the depiction of living creatures, on the grounds that "it implies a likeness to the creative activity of God".<sup id="cite_ref-AG_126-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-AG-126">[124]</a></sup> The origins of this new pictorial tradition are uncertain, but Arabic illustrated manuscripts such as the _[Maqamat al-Hariri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maqamat_al-Hariri "Maqamat al-Hariri")_ shared many characteristics with Christian [Syriac](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syriac_Christianity "Syriac Christianity") illustrated manuscripts, such as [Syriac Gospels (British Library, Add. 7170)](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syriac_Gospels,_British_Library,_Add._7170 "Syriac Gospels, British Library, Add. 7170").<sup id="cite_ref-MET_161-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-MET-161">[159]</a></sup> This synthesis seems to point to a common pictorial tradition which developed from circa 1180 CE in the region, which was highly influenced by [Byzantine art](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byzantine_art "Byzantine art").<sup id="cite_ref-MET_161-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-MET-161">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnelders20101–2_162-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnelders20101%E2%80%932-162">[160]</a></sup>

### Metalwork [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Metalwork")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Celestial_Globe_with_Stand,_Iran,_1144-45,_Louvre.jpg)

Seljuk celestial globe with stand, Iran, 1144–45, [Louvre Museum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louvre_Museum "Louvre Museum"). The globe mentions: "This globe includes all the stars mentioned in the book of the _[Almagest](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Almagest "Almagest")_ after modifying them in proportion with the interval between the calculations of [Ptolemy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ptolemy "Ptolemy") and the year \[A.H.\] 540, i.e. 1144. \[It is t\]he work of (san‘at) Yunis b. al-Husayn al-Asturlabi \[in the\] year 539".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016195,_object_116_163-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016195,_object_116-163">[161]</a></sup>

Starting around the middle of the 12th century, there appears to have been a major increase in the number of artistic metalwork objects produced in the eastern Islamic world (roughly Iran and Central Asia).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001166–167_164-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001166%E2%80%93167-164">[162]</a></sup> More of these objects have survived from after the 1140s than from before this period.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup> The major centers of production were initially concentrated in the Khorasan region, including [Nishapur](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nishapur "Nishapur"), [Herat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herat "Herat"), and [Ghazna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghazni "Ghazni").<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup> There is some scholarly debate about the patronage of these objects,<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup> with some suggesting that the growth in production is explainable by the growth of a bourgeoisie in Khorasan which had the means to afford such costly craftsmanship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001166–167_164-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001166%E2%80%93167-164">[162]</a></sup> This is attested in part by inscriptions naming merchant owners, but most surviving objects are nonetheless attributed by their inscriptions to sultans, royal household members, or state officials.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup> In the early 13th century, this expansion of metalwork art and patronage also occurred further west, in the Levant and Mesopotamia, under the successors of the Great Seljuks (the Zengids, the Artuqids, and the Anatolian Seljuks), spurred in part by the immigration of metalworkers from Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001244_166-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001244-166">[164]</a></sup>

Made of bronze or brass, objects could also be [inlaid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inlay "Inlay") with copper and silver. This latter technique had fallen out of fashion in previous centuries but it underwent a revival that probably originated in Khorasan during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup> The forms produced include both traditional Khorasani types, such as fluted [ewers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pitcher_(container) "Pitcher (container)") and hooded [incense burners](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Censer "Censer"), and newe shapes, such as penboxes with rounded ends and candlesticks with drum-like bodies.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup> Many metal vessels also featured [zoomorphic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zoomorphism "Zoomorphism") forms. The most sophisticated works were created by [raising](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raising_(metalworking) "Raising (metalworking)") and [sinking](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinking_(metalworking) "Sinking (metalworking)"), with decoration executed in [_repoussé_](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repouss%C3%A9_and_chasing "Repoussé and chasing"). To create some shapes, multiple metal sheets were carefully soldered together, with the seams made invisible by various means, such as by camouflaging them under decorative friezes.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup> The wide range of ornamental motifs include [arabesques](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabesque "Arabesque"), [geometric designs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_geometric_patterns "Islamic geometric patterns"), real and mythological animals, and even scenes of human figures such as musicians and horsemen.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup> Arabic inscriptions are found on almost all metalwork art objects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001167_167-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEttinghausenGrabarJenkins-Madina2001167-167">[165]</a></sup> An innovation of this period, almost exclusive to metalwork, is the rendering of Arabic script into figurative forms. The earliest example of this is found on the so-called [Bobrinsky Bucket](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bobrinski_Bucket "Bobrinski Bucket").<sup id="cite_ref-:24_165-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:24-165">[163]</a></sup>

-   "A demonstration of the excellence achieved in metalwork under the Seljuqs": bronze incense burner shaped like lion, with removable head, dated 1181–82 CE, [Taybad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taybad "Taybad"), Iran. (Metropolitan Museum of Art)<sup id="cite_ref-168"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup>
    
-   Gold roundel, 11th century Iran. It "exemplifies the refinement of Seljuq goldsmithing".<sup id="cite_ref-170"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup>
    
-   Seljuk gold necklace, 11th century Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-172"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup>
    
-   [Bobrinsky Bucket](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bobrinski_Bucket "Bobrinski Bucket"), a bronze cauldron decorated with human figures. From 1163 CE, Herat, Afghanistan. ([Hermitage Museum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermitage_Museum "Hermitage Museum"))<sup id="cite_ref-174"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup>
    
-   Brass candlestick with _[repoussé](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repouss%C3%A9_and_chasing "Repoussé and chasing")_ work. Inlaid with silver, copper, and black bitumen. Late 12th century, Afghanistan. ([Freer Gallery of Art](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freer_Gallery_of_Art "Freer Gallery of Art"))<sup id="cite_ref-175"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup>
    

### Textiles and clothing [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Textiles and clothing")

Seljuk period silk robe in Persian style, with birds motifs in medallions (11th-12th century).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock201695–96,_object_24_176-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock201695%E2%80%9396,_object_24-176">[174]</a></sup>

Figures in Arab dress, with long robes, turbans and bare or sandalled feet, reading books. _[Kitāb al-Diryāq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kit%C4%81b_al-Diry%C4%81q "Kitāb al-Diryāq")_, [Jazira](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jazira_region "Jazira region"), 1198 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-CLOTHING_159-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-CLOTHING-159">[157]</a></sup>

The general clothing style attributed to the Seljuks is that of the _[aqbiya turkiyya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qaba "Qaba")_, or long robe or decorated caftan with “Turkish” cut, with a front opening closing diagonally from right to left.<sup id="cite_ref-MET98_177-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-MET98-177">[175]</a></sup> Patterned textiles were used, together with _[tiraz](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tiraz "Tiraz")_ bands on the upper sleeves. Clothing included tall boots, as well as various hats of the _[sharbush](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sharbush "Sharbush")_ type, often including a fur lining.<sup id="cite_ref-EI_Clothing_127-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-EI_Clothing-127">[125]</a></sup> These styles continued during the 13th century in the smaller Turkic successor states, such as the [Zengids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zengids "Zengids") or [Artuqids](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artuqids "Artuqids"), where many more illustrations are available, especially in manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-EI_Clothing_127-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-EI_Clothing-127">[125]</a></sup>

On the other hand, the affluent sedentary Persian population seems to have adopted different robe styles, with a front opening closing diagonally from left to right, called the _aqbiya tatariyya_ or "Tatar style", but actually also characteristic of Persian caftans from the last decades of the Sasanian dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-MET98_177-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-MET98-177">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EI_Clothing_127-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-EI_Clothing-127">[125]</a></sup> The fabrics represent what could be called a "[Sasanian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sasanian_Empire "Sasanian Empire") renaissance", with styles going back to the Sasanian or Sogdian period.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_178-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:12-178">[176]</a></sup> Seljuk fabrics are often distinguished by the representation of nature, by minimal ornamental details, and by the combination of colorful linens giving an interchangeable color effect to the fabric. Many realistic natural elements characterize the composition of the fabrics, such as animals and plants, forming patterns consisting of arabesque elements.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_178-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-:12-178">[176]</a></sup>

In many manuscripts of the period, great care is taken to distinghuish the clothing of figures of power and authority in Seljuk style, from that of the otherwise omnipresent figures in Arabic or local style with their long robes, turbans and bare or sandalled feet.<sup id="cite_ref-AS_179-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-AS-179">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FB232_180-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FB232-180">[178]</a></sup> According to Snelders:

> In a number of these manuscripts a careful distinction is made between royal and non-royal figures, both in terms of physical appearance and dress. Whereas princes and governors are commonly represented with the same ‘Asiatic’ or ‘Oriental’ facial features, and dressed in Turkish military garments like fur-trimmed caps (_[sharbush](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sharbush "Sharbush")_) and short close-fitting tunics, most other figures are depicted with ‘Arab’ or ‘Semitic’ facial features, and dressed in long robes and turbans. Apparently in keeping with the contemporary political and social makeup of the region in which these manuscripts were produced, a visual distinction was made along ethnic and social lines, between the non-Arab Turkish ruling elite and the indigenous Arab bourgeoisie.
> 
> — Snelders _Identity and Christian-Muslim interaction : medieval art of the Syrian Orthodox from the Mosul area_.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESnelders2010Paragraph_4.7_181-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnelders2010Paragraph_4.7-181">[179]</a></sup>

## Legacy [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Legacy")

The dynasty brought revival, energy, and reunion to the Islamic civilization hitherto dominated by Arabs and Persians. The Seljuks founded universities and were also patrons of art and literature. Their reign is characterized by [Persian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_people "Persian people") astronomers such as [Omar Khayyám](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omar_Khayy%C3%A1m "Omar Khayyám"), and the [Persian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_people "Persian people") philosopher [al-Ghazali](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Ghazali "Al-Ghazali"). Under the Seljuks, [New Persian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Persian "New Persian") became the language for historical recording, while the center of Arabic language culture shifted from Baghdad to [Cairo](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairo "Cairo").<sup id="cite_ref-182"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup>

## Notes [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Notes")

1.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-16 "Jump up")** In order to distinguish it from the [Anatolian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatolia "Anatolia") branch of the [Seljuk dynasty](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_dynasty "Seljuk dynasty"), the [Sultanate of Rum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum "Sultanate of Rum").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMecit2014128_14-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMecit2014128-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeacockYıldız20136_15-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeacockY%C4%B1ld%C4%B1z20136-15">[15]</a></sup>

1.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-69 "Jump up")** Here, Iraq is meant in its medieval sense, which incorporated western Iran (historic 'Iraq al-'Ajam or Persian Iraq, also known as [Jibal](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jibal "Jibal")) as well as 'Iraq al-Arab (Arab Iraq), roughly the central and southern parts of present-day Iraq

## Footnotes [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Seljuk_Empire&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Footnotes")

1.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-226 "Jump up")** Grand Vizier [Sāhīp Shams ad-Dīn Īsfahānī](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shams_al-Din_Isfahani "Shams al-Din Isfahani") ruled the country on behalf of ʿIzz ad-Dīn [Kay Kāwus II](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaykaus_II "Kaykaus II") between 1246 and 1249
2.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-227 "Jump up")** Grand Vizier [Parwāna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perv%C3%A2ne "Pervâne") [Mu'in al-Din Suleyman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mu%27in_al-Din_Parwana "Mu'in al-Din Parwana") ruled the country on behalf of Ghiyāth ad-Dīn [Kay Khusraw III](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keyhusrev_III "Keyhusrev III") between 1266 and 2 August 1277 (1 [Rabi' al-awwal](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rabi%27_al-awwal "Rabi' al-awwal") 676)
3.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-229 "Jump up")** Between 1246 and 1249 ʿIzz ad-Dīn [Kay Kāwus II](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaykaus_II "Kaykaus II") reigned alone
4.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-230 "Jump up")** ʿIzz ad-Dīn [Kay Kāwus II](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaykaus_II "Kaykaus II") was defeated on October 14, 1256 in [Sultanhanı](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sultanhan%C4%B1 "Sultanhanı") ([Sultan Han](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sultan_Han "Sultan Han"), [Aksaray](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aksaray "Aksaray")) and he acceded to the throne on May 1, 1257 again after the departure of [Baiju Noyan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baiju_Noyan "Baiju Noyan") from [Anatolia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatolia "Anatolia")
5.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-231 "Jump up")** Between 1262 and 1266 Rukn ad-Dīn [Kilij Arslan IV](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilij_Arslan_IV "Kilij Arslan IV") reigned alone
6.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-232 "Jump up")** Between 1249 and 1254 triple reign of three brothers
7.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-233 "Jump up")** According to [İbn Bîbî](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Bibi "Ibn Bibi"), _[el-Evâmirü'l-ʿAlâʾiyye](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=El-Ev%C3%A2mir%C3%BC%27l-%CA%BFAl%C3%A2%CA%BEiyye&action=edit&redlink=1 "El-Evâmirü'l-ʿAlâʾiyye (page does not exist)") \[[tr](https://tr.wikipedia.org/wiki/el-Ev%C3%A2mir%C3%BC%27l-%CA%BFAl%C3%A2%CA%BEiyye "tr:el-Evâmirü'l-ʿAlâʾiyye")\] fi'l-umûri'l-Alâiyye_, p. 727. (10 [Dhu al-Hijjah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah "Dhu al-Hijjah") 675 – 17 [Muharram](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muharram "Muharram") 676)
8.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-234 "Jump up")** According to Yazıcıoğlu Ali, _[Tevârih-i Âl-i Selçuk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tev%C3%A2rih-i_%C3%82l-i_Sel%C3%A7uk "Tevârih-i Âl-i Selçuk") \[[tr](https://tr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tev%C3%A2rih-i_%C3%82l-i_Sel%C3%A7uk "tr:Tevârih-i Âl-i Selçuk")\]_ p. 62. (10 [Dhu al-Hijjah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah "Dhu al-Hijjah") 677 – 17 [Muharram](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muharram "Muharram") 678)

It derives from Selčiik, born Toqaq Temir Yally, a war-lord (sil-baši), from the Qiniq tribal grouping of the Oghuz. Seljuk, in the rough and tumble of internal Oghuz politics, fled to Jand, c. 985, after falling out with his overlord.

In the tenth century, these and other nomadic tribes, often collectively referred to as Turkomans, migrated out of Central Asia and into Iran. Turkish tribes initially served as mercenary soldiers for local rulers but soon set up their own kingdoms in Iran, some of which grew into Empires – most notably the Great Seljuk Empire. In the meantime, many Turkic rulers and tribespeople eventually converted to Islam.

Farther east, medieval Turco-Iranian military patronage states, such as those of the Ghaznavids, Seljuks, Timurids, and early Ottomans, appear to have been more directly affected by the banner traditions of the nomadic Turkic and Mongol populations of the Central Asian steppes, who in turn were influenced by the traditions of the various empires and kingdoms that ruled China, Japan, and Korea.

19.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-:9_20-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-:9_20-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-:9_20-2) [<sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-:9_20-3) Bloom, Jonathan M.; Blair, Sheila S. (2009), ["Saljuq"](https://www.oxfordreference.com/display/10.1093/acref/9780195309911.001.0001/acref-9780195309911-e-807), _The Grove Encyclopedia of Islamic Art and Architecture_, Oxford University Press, [doi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1093/acref/9780195309911.001.0001](https://doi.org/10.1093%2Facref%2F9780195309911.001.0001), [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-19-530991-1](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-530991-1 "Special:BookSources/978-0-19-530991-1"), retrieved 2023-01-01
20.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-21 "Jump up")** [_Court and Cosmos: The Great Age of the Seljuqs - MetPublications - The Metropolitan Museum of Art_](https://www.metmuseum.org/art/metpublications/Court_and_Cosmos). Metropolitan Museum of Art. 2016. p. 33. The Seljuqs were the first in a long series of Turkish dynasties that dominated the Middle East up to the early twentieth century; among the most famous were the Ottomans (ca. 1300–1923) in Anatolia, the Balkans, and the Arab lands; and the Timurids (1370–ca. 1507), Safavids (1501–1722), and Qajars (1785–1925) in Iran. The Seljuq synthesis of Turkish traditions with Iranian and Islamic ones was emulated, to varying degrees, by all these dynasties. Institutions first introduced to the Middle East by the Seljuqs were also adopted by these later dynasties.

46.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-XX_47-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-XX_47-1) [_Encyclopaedia Iranica, Ghurids_](https://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/ghurids). The actual fighting in Khorasan at this time was largely between the Ghurids and Tekeš's brother Solṭānšāh, who had carved out for himself personally a principality in western Khorasan, until in 586/1190. Ḡīāṯ-al–Dīn and Moʿezz-al-Dīn defeated Solṭānšāh near Marv in 588/1192, captured him, and took over his territories (Jūzjānī, I, 303-4, tr. I, pp. 246-47). When Tekeš died in 596/1200 (Ebn al-Aṯīr, Beirut, XII, pp. 156-58), Ḡīāṯ-al-Dīn was able to take over most of the towns of Khorasan as far west as Besṭām in Qūmes.

73.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-75 "Jump up")**  • _[Encyclopaedia Iranica](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclopaedia_Iranica "Encyclopaedia Iranica")_, "[Šahrbānu](http://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/sahrbanu)", Online Edition: "here one might bear in mind that non-Persian dynasties such as the Ghaznavids, Saljuqs and Ilkhanids were rapidly to adopt the Persian language and have their origins traced back to the ancient kings of Persia rather than to Turkish heroes or Muslim saints ..."  • Josef W. Meri, "Medieval Islamic Civilization: An Encyclopedia", Routledge, 2005, p. 399  • Michael Mandelbaum, "Central Asia and the World", Council on Foreign Relations (May 1994), p. 79  • Jonathan Dewald, "Europe 1450 to 1789: Encyclopedia of the Early Modern World", Charles Scribner's Sons, 2004, p. 24: "Turcoman armies coming from the East had driven the Byzantines out of much of Asia Minor and established the Persianized sultanate of the Seljuks."
74.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-76 "Jump up")** \*C.E. Bosworth, "Turkmen Expansion towards the west" in _UNESCO History of Humanity_, Volume IV, titled "From the Seventh to the Sixteenth Century", UNESCO Publishing / Routledge, p. 391: "While the Arabic language retained its primacy in such spheres as law, theology and science, the culture of the Seljuk court and secular literature within the sultanate became largely Persianized; this is seen in the early adoption of Persian epic names by the Seljuk rulers (Qubād, Kay Khusraw and so on) and in the use of Persian as a literary language (Turkmen must have been essentially a vehicle for everyday speech at this time). The process of Persianization accelerated in the thirteenth century with the presence in Konya of two of the most distinguished refugees fleeing before the Mongols, Bahā' al-Dīn Walad and his son Mawlānā Jalāl al-Dīn Rūmī, whose _Mathnawī_, composed in Konya, constitutes one of the crowning glories of classical Persian literature."  • Mehmed Fuad Köprülü, "Early Mystics in Turkish Literature", Translated by Gary Leiser and [Robert Dankoff](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Dankoff "Robert Dankoff"), Routledge, 2006, p. 149: "If we wish to sketch, in broad outline, the civilization created by the Seljuks of Anatolia, we must recognize that the local—i.e., non-Muslim, element was fairly insignificant compared to the Turkish and Arab-Persian elements, and that the Persian element was paramount. The Seljuk rulers, to be sure, who were in contact with not only Muslim Persian civilization, but also with the Arab civilizations in al-jazlra and Syria—indeed, with all Muslim peoples as far as India—also had connections with {various} Byzantine courts. Some of these rulers, like the great 'Ala' al-Dln Kai-Qubad I himself, who married Byzantine princesses and thus strengthened relations with their neighbors to the west, lived for many years in Byzantium and became very familiar with the customs and ceremonial at the Byzantine court. Still, this close contact with the ancient Greco-Roman and Christian traditions only resulted in their adoption of a policy of tolerance toward art, aesthetic life, painting, music, independent thought—in short, toward those things that were frowned upon by the narrow and piously ascetic views {of their subjects}. The contact of the common people with the Greeks and Armenians had basically the same result. \[Before coming to Anatolia,\] the Turkmens had been in contact with many nations and had long shown their ability to synthesize the artistic elements that they had adopted from these nations. When they settled in Anatolia, they encountered peoples with whom they had not yet been in contact and immediately established relations with them as well. Ala al-Din Kai-Qubad I established ties with the Genoese and, especially, the Venetians at the ports of Sinop and Antalya, which belonged to him, and granted them commercial and legal concessions. Meanwhile, the Mongol invasion, which caused a great number of scholars and artisans to flee from Turkmenistan, Iran, and Khwarazm and settle within the Empire of the Seljuks of Anatolia, resulted in a reinforcing of Persian influence on the Anatolian Turks. Indeed, despite all claims to the contrary, there is no question that Persian influence was paramount among the Seljuks of Anatolia. This is clearly revealed by the fact that the sultans who ascended the throne after Ghiyath al-Din Kai-Khusraw I assumed titles taken from ancient Persian mythology, like Kai-Khusraw, Kai-Ka us, and Kai-Qubad; and that. Ala' al-Din Kai-Qubad I had some passages from the Shahname inscribed on the walls of Konya and Sivas. When we take into consideration domestic life in the Konya courts and the sincerity of the favor and attachment of the rulers to Persian poets and Persian literature, then this fact \[i.e., the importance of Persian influence\] is undeniable. With regard to the private lives of the rulers, their amusements, and palace ceremonial, the most definite influence was also that of Iran, mixed with the early Turkish traditions, and not that of Byzantium."
    
    -   Stephen P. Blake, _[Shahjahanabad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shahjahanabad "Shahjahanabad"): The Sovereign City in Mughal India, 1639–1739_. Cambridge University Press, 1991. pg 123: "For the Seljuks and Il-Khanids in Iran it was the rulers rather than the conquered who were "Persianized and Islamicized"
    
75.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-77 "Jump up")** \*_[Encyclopaedia Iranica](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclopaedia_Iranica "Encyclopaedia Iranica")_, "[Šahrbānu](http://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/sahrbanu)", Online Edition: "here one might bear in mind that non-Persian dynasties such as the Ghaznavids, Saljuqs and Ilkhanids were rapidly to adopt the Persian language and have their origins traced back to the ancient kings of Persia rather than to Turkish heroes or Muslim saints ..."
    
    -   O.Özgündenli, "[Persian Manuscripts in Ottoman and Modern Turkish Libraries](http://www.iranica.com/newsite/articles/ot_grp7/ot_pers_mss_ott_20050106.html) [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20120122005207/http://www.iranica.com/newsite/articles/ot_grp7/ot_pers_mss_ott_20050106.html) 2012-01-22 at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine")", _[Encyclopaedia Iranica](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclopaedia_Iranica "Encyclopaedia Iranica")_, Online Edition
    -   M. Ravandi, "The Seljuq court at Konya and the Persianisation of Anatolian Cities", in _Mesogeios (Mediterranean Studies)_, vol. 25-6 (2005), pp. 157–69
    -   F. Daftary, "Sectarian and National Movements in Iran, Khorasan, and Trasoxania during Umayyad and Early Abbasid Times", in _History of Civilizations of Central Asia_, Vol 4, pt. 1; edited by M.S. Asimov and [C.E. Bosworth](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clifford_Edmund_Bosworth "Clifford Edmund Bosworth"); [UNESCO](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNESCO "UNESCO") Publishing, [Institute of Ismaili Studies](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institute_of_Ismaili_Studies "Institute of Ismaili Studies"): "Not only did the inhabitants of Khurasan not succumb to the language of the nomadic invaders, but they imposed their own tongue on them. The region could even assimilate the Turkic Ghaznavids and Seljuks (eleventh and twelfth centuries), the Timurids (fourteenth–fifteenth centuries), and the Qajars (nineteenth–twentieth centuries) ..."
    
76.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-78 "Jump up")** "The [Turko-Persian tradition](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turko-Persian_tradition "Turko-Persian tradition") features Persian culture patronized by Turkic rulers." See Daniel Pipes: "The Event of Our Era: Former Soviet Muslim Republics Change the Middle East" in Michael Mandelbaum, "Central Asia and the World: Kazakhstan, Uzbekistan, Tajikistan, Kyrgyzstan, Turkemenistan and the World", Council on Foreign Relations, p. 79. Exact statement: "In Short, the Turko-Persian tradition featured Persian culture patronized by Turcophone rulers."

84.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-86 "Jump up")** \*_An Introduction to the History of the Turkic Peoples_ (Peter B. Golden. Otto Harrasowitz, 1992). pg 386: "Turkic penetration probably began in the Hunnic era and its aftermath. Steady pressure from Turkic nomads was typical of the Khazar era, although there are no unambiguous references to permanent settlements. These most certainly occurred with the arrival of the Oguz in the 11th century. The Turkicization of much of Azarbayjan, according to Soviet scholars, was completed largely during the Ilxanid period if not by late Seljuk times. Sumer, placing a slightly different emphasis on the data (more correct in my view), posts three periods which Turkicization took place: Seljuk, Mongol and Post-Mongol (Qara Qoyunlu, Aq Qoyunlu and Safavid). In the first two, Oguz Turkic tribes advanced or were driven to the western frontiers (Anatolia) and Northern Azarbaijan (Arran, the Mugan steppe). In the last period, the Turkic elements in Iran (derived from Oguz, with lesser admixture of Uygur, Qipchaq, Qaluq and other Turks brought to Iran during the Chinggisid era, as well as Turkicized Mongols) were joined now by Anatolian Turks migrating back to Iran. This marked the final stage of Turkicization. Although there is some evidence for the presence of Qipchaqs among the Turkic tribes coming to this region, there is little doubt that the critical mass which brought about this linguistic shift was provided by the same Oguz-Turkmen tribes that had come to Anatolia. The Azeris of today are an overwhelmingly sedentary, detribalized people. Anthropologically, they are little distinguished from the Iranian neighbors."
    
    -   John Perry: "We should distinguish two complementary ways in which the advent of the Turks affected the language map of Iran. First, since the Turkish-speaking rulers of most Iranian polities from the Ghaznavids and Seljuks onward were already Iranized and patronized Persian literature in their domains, the expansion of Turk-ruled empires served to expand the territorial domain of written Persian into the conquered areas, notably Anatolia and Central and South Asia. Secondly, the influx of massive Turkish-speaking populations (culminating with the rank and file of the Mongol armies) and their settlement in large areas of Iran (particularly in Azerbaijan and the northwest), progressively turkicized local speakers of Persian, Kurdish and other Iranian languages"
    
    (John Perry. "The Historical Role of Turkish in Relation to Persian of Iran". _Iran & the Caucasus_, Vol. 5, (2001), pp. 193–200.)
    
    -   According to C.E. Bosworth:
    
    "The eastern Caucasus came under Saljuq control in the middle years of the 5th/11th century, and in c. 468/1075-56 Sultan Alp Arslān sent his slave commander ʿEmād-al-dīn Savtigin as governor of Azerbaijan and Arrān, displacing the last Shaddadids. From this period begins the increasing Turkicization of Arrān, under the Saljuqs and then under the line of Eldigüzid or Ildeñizid Atabegs, who had to defend eastern Transcaucasia against the attacks of the resurgent Georgian kings. The influx of Oghuz and other Türkmens was accentuated by the Mongol invasions. Bardaʿa had never revived fully after the Rūs sacking, and is little mentioned in the sources." (C.E. Bsowrth, Arran in _Encyclopædia Iranica_)
    
    -   According to Fridrik Thordarson:
    
    "Iranian influence on Caucasian languages. There is general agreement that Iranian languages predominated in Azerbaijan from the 1st millennium b.c. until the advent of the Turks in a.d. the 11th century (see Menges, pp. 41–42; Camb. Hist. Iran IV, pp. 226–228, and VI, pp. 950–952). The process of Turkicization was essentially complete by the beginning of the 16th century, and today Iranian languages are spoken in only a few scattered settlements in the area."

111.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-AF_113-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-AF_113-1) Falk, Avner (8 May 2018). 112.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-JH_114-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-JH_114-1) McHugo, John (2 April 2018). 113.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-115 "Jump up")** Rice, David Talbot (1965). [_Islamic Art (Volume 20 of World of art)_](https://www.islamicmanuscripts.info/reference/books/Rice-1965-Islamic-Art/Rice-1965-Islamic-Art-096-115.pdf) (PDF). London: Thames and Hudson.
114.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-BK_116-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-BK_116-1) Küçüksipahioğlu, Birsel (30 June 2020). ["Musul ve Halep Valisi İmâdeddin Zengi'nin Haçlılarla Mücadelesi"](https://iupress.istanbul.edu.tr/en/journal/jos/article/musul-ve-halep-valisi-imadeddin-zenginin-haclilarla-mucadelesi). _Journal of Oriental Studies_ (36): 103–126. [doi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.26650/jos.2020.005](https://doi.org/10.26650%2Fjos.2020.005). Staying in Mosul until the death of Sultān [Muhammad Tapar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad_I_Tapar "Muhammad I Tapar") in 1118, Zangi then entered the service of the Sultān's son and the new Seljuk ruler [Mahmūd](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahmud_II_(Seljuk_sultan) "Mahmud II (Seljuk sultan)") (1118-1119), remaining loyal to him to the end. With the new era introduced with the defeat of Sultān Mahmūd in the Sāveh battle he engaged his uncle [Sanjar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahmad_Sanjar "Ahmad Sanjar") in 1119, which opened the way for Sanjar (1119-1157) to accede to the throne of Great Seljuk Empire, Mahmūd was assigned to the Iraqi Seljuk Sultānate (1119-1131), continuing his rule there. In 1124, Sultān Mahmūd granted the city of Wasit to Imad al-Din Zangi as a ıqta, and conferred him the Military Governorship of Basra together with Baghdad and Iraq in 1127. The reason behind such assignments was to attempt to impede Abbasid Caliph al-Mustarshid (1118-1135) who then wished to build a worldwide dominance. Indeed, the efforts of Zangi in the fight of Mahmūd, whom Sanjar urgently sent to Baghdad, against the Caliph ensured the Sultān became victorious, and he contributed to the efforts in damaging the sole authority and dominance claims of the Caliph. Following the deaths of Mosul Governor Aq-Sunqur el-Porsuqi and his successor and son Mas'ud in the same year in 1127, Zangi was appointed Governor of Mosul. He was also in charge of al-Jazeera and Northern Syria, and Sultān Mahmūd approved him being assigned as the Atabeg of his two sons, Farrukh shāh and Alparslan. Thus the Atabegdom of Mosul was formed.

124.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-AG_126-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-AG_126-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-AG_126-2) George, Alain (February 2012). 125.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-EI_Clothing_127-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-EI_Clothing_127-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-EI_Clothing_127-2) [<sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-EI_Clothing_127-3) Peck, Elsie H. 126.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-128 "Jump up")** [Canby et al. 2016](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#CITEREFCanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016), p. xiii: "While one could quibble that the art could not be Seljuq if their dynasty was no longer in power, we argue that the twenty-five years after the end, in 1194, of the Great Seljuq dynasty in Iran marked a continuation of the styles and techniques in various media that had been established during Seljuq rule. Likewise, through their architecture and luxury objects, the Seljuqs of Rum and their atabegs and successors in Anatolia, the Jazira, and Syria had a lasting impact on the art of those areas until the early fourteenth century. Their distinctive choice of iconography is found on all forms of the visual arts associated with them, revealing the strength of their cultural contribution."
127.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHillenbrand2010118,_note_10_129-0 "Jump up")** [Hillenbrand 2010](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#CITEREFHillenbrand2010), p. 118, note 10.
128.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-AC_130-0 "Jump up")** [Contadini 2012](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#CITEREFContadini2012), pp. 126, 127, 155 P.126: “Official” Turkish figures wear a standard combination of a sharbūsh, a three-quarters length robe, and boots. Arab figures, in contrast, have different headgear (usually a turban), a robe that is either full-length or, if three-quarters length, has baggy trousers below, and they usually wear flat shoes or (...) go barefoot (...) P.127: Reference has already been made to the combination of boots and _[sharbūsh](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sharb%C5%ABsh "Sharbūsh")_ as markers of official status (...) the combination is standard, even being reflected in thirteenth-century Coptic paintings, and serves to distinguish, in Grabar’s formulation, the world of the Turkish ruler and that of the Arab. (...) The type worn by the official figures in the 1237 Maqāmāt, depicted, for example, on fol. 59r,67 consists of a gold cap surmounted by a little round top and with fur trimming creating a triangular area at the front which either shows the gold cap or is a separate plaque. A particular imposing example in this manuscript is the massive _sharbūsh_ with much more fur than usual that is worn by the princely official on the right frontispiece on fol. 1v.}}

157.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-CLOTHING_159-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-CLOTHING_159-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-CLOTHING_159-2) Shahbazi, Shapur (30 August 2020). ["CLOTHING"](https://referenceworks.brillonline.com/entries/encyclopaedia-iranica-online/clothing-COM_7759?lang=en). _Encyclopaedia Iranica Online_. Brill. Nevertheless, the most distinctive feature of late Saljuq and post-Saljuq male dress was the popularity of patterned textiles for these garments. (...) That these patterns do not merely represent ceramic conventions is clear from the rendering of garments in fragmentary wall paintings and in illustrations from the copy of Varqa wa Golšāh already mentioned, as well as in frontispieces to the volumes of Abu'l-Faraj Eṣfahānī's Ketāb al-aḡānī dated 614-16/1217-19 and to two copies of Ketāb al-deryāq (Book of antidotes) by Pseudo-Galen, dated 596/1199 and ascribed to the second quarter of the 7th/13th century respectively (Survey of Persian Art V, pl. 554A-B; Ateş, pls. 1/3, 6/16, 18; D. S. Rice, 1953, figs. 14-19; Ettinghausen, 1962, pp. 65, 85, 91). The last three manuscripts, all of them attributed to northern Mesopotamia, show that the stiff coat with diagonal closing and arm bands was also worn in that region from the end of the 6th/12th century.

174.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock201695%E2%80%9396,_object_24_176-0 "Jump up")** [Canby et al. 2016](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#CITEREFCanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016), pp. 95–96, object 24.
175.  ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-MET98_177-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#cite_ref-MET98_177-1) [Canby et al. 2016](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_Empire#CITEREFCanbyBeyazitRugiadiPeacock2016), p. 98: "Paradoxically, however, while the style commonly attributed to the Seljuqs is that of the left-buttoning aqbiya turkiyya, or “Turkish” cut, and although this is the prevailing style of robe in illustrations from contemporary manuscripts, there is perhaps only a single complete robe attributed to the Seljuq period that closes (...) Taken within the context of adab, however, it becomes possible that the adoption of the “Tatar-style” coat was a conscious decision on the part of the affluent sedentary Persian population, as it was less identifiable with the Seljuq Turks. These associations would have been increasingly acute toward the end of the Seljuq period, as a result of the Khwarazm Shah invasions and perhaps exacerbated by the westward migrations of nomadic groups at the start of the Mongol invasion."

[_Late Antiquity: Eastern Perspectives_](https://www.academia.edu/1298503). Oxford: Oxbow. pp. 145–163. [ISBN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-906094-53-2](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-906094-53-2 "Special:BookSources/978-0-906094-53-2").
-   Van Renterghem, Vanessa (2015). "Baghdad: A View from the Edge on the Seljuk Empire". In Herzig, Edmund; Stewart, Sarah (eds.). _The Age of the Seljuqs: The Idea of Iran_. Vol. VI. I.B. Tauris.
